<div id="review_2087522161" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2087522161" />
    <a title="Nicola" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4596610-nicola"><img alt="Nicola" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291056380p2/4596610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2087522161?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicola" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicola" href="/user/show/4596610-nicola">Nicola</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140189377" href="/book/show/149877.Seize_the_Day">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596610-nicola?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2087522161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12576244652826570947">2 1/2 stars<br/><br/>My first <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow' title='Saul Bellow' rel='nofollow noopener'>Saul Bellow</a> and not really to my taste. It's not badly written, I can see why he is a respected author, but, no, just not my bag. It reminded me a little bit of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman' title='Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death of a Salesman</a> in its depressing tone as Tommy Wilhem is another American Man driven on by the demons of social and financial success and the pressures of family expectations. It was all very moving but I don't really enjoy bleak stories like this - I 'enjoyed' <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman' title='Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death of a Salesman</a> but after studying it in school </span>
  <span id="freeText12576244652826570947" style="display:none">2 1/2 stars<br /><br />My first <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow" title="Saul Bellow" rel="nofollow noopener">Saul Bellow</a> and not really to my taste. It's not badly written, I can see why he is a respected author, but, no, just not my bag. It reminded me a little bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman" title="Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Death of a Salesman</a> in its depressing tone as Tommy Wilhem is another American Man driven on by the demons of social and financial success and the pressures of family expectations. It was all very moving but I don't really enjoy bleak stories like this - I 'enjoyed' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman" title="Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Death of a Salesman</a> but after studying it in school I was so put off reading anything more by Miller that it was about 20 years before I read/listened to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17250.The_Crucible" title="The Crucible by Arthur Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crucible</a> and that only after I was assured by someone whose judgement I trust that it was nothing like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman" title="Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Death of a Salesman</a>. <br /><br />Tommy Wilhem, our 'hero' has had some financial success in his life but it's not really enough. He has left his wife and is being led around with promises of a divorce while disgorging money hand over fist. Then he encounters work problems and leaves his old job so he is now unemployed and nearly completely broke. His father refuses to help him and he decides that his only option is to try to earn a living on the financial markets under the tutelage of a rather dubious new friend. It's probably not going to end well but there you have the hopeful optimism of the desperate.<br /><br />After reading this I wondered if the reason that I preferred British books over American ones as a general rule was the predilection for some American writers to focus on the social drive for money in this way. Of course the pursuit of money and status is pretty universal but it occurred to me that American male writers do produce a lot of these grey grimy books for whatever reasons. <br /><br />I'm probably just over thinking it. But I think I'll shelve my plans for reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703.The_Plot_Against_America" title="The Plot Against America by Philip Roth" rel="nofollow noopener">The Plot Against America</a> for now. I'm just not a fan of really depressing books. (Runs off to read: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15905.Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy" rel="nofollow noopener">Thomas Hardy</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4750._mile_Zola" title="Émile Zola" rel="nofollow noopener">Émile Zola</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16.Edith_Wharton" title="Edith Wharton" rel="nofollow noopener">Edith Wharton</a>)</span>
  <a data-text-id="12576244652826570947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_56756451" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56756451" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/141982-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442471947p2/141982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56756451?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/141982-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780606310925" href="/book/show/149881.Seize_the_Day">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56756451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4402493566200213731">Apolgies in advance for skipping over the plot summary, but here's what I think I learned from this book:<br/><br/>1) Bellow, like Banville, is a master of characterization, the expression of character through movement, reaction, idiosyncrasies, etc. It's not just what they look like and what they're wearing (though this is important) it's what these things say about the character and how they're expressed through speech, interaction with others, moments of isolation, etc. It can't be all wooden descripti</span>
  <span id="freeText4402493566200213731" style="display:none">Apolgies in advance for skipping over the plot summary, but here's what I think I learned from this book:<br /><br />1) Bellow, like Banville, is a master of characterization, the expression of character through movement, reaction, idiosyncrasies, etc. It's not just what they look like and what they're wearing (though this is important) it's what these things say about the character and how they're expressed through speech, interaction with others, moments of isolation, etc. It can't be all wooden descriptions of the external or the seething turmoil of the internal. If you want a reader to emotionally connect with your character you have to do both. Not just once or twice, but always. In a sense, Bellow is never not doing this and that's how the story advances. <br /> <br />2) The unlikable hero. Wilhelm is repugnant, utterly so. He fits the profile of a man about to hit bottom and though it's plain that he needs to embrace change he can't (or won't) until the collapse, which is inevitable, comes. Everyone around him acts consistently: his father finds him repugnant, the conman fleeces him, his estranged wife browbeats him -- as we would certainly do if we were in these positions. Wilhelm's personal habits are despicable, his weaknesses profound, he is incapable of change; however, his desire for it makes him somehow sympathetic. He wants what we all want: enough to get by on and to put our problems behind us, but instead of the high roads he takes the low, which ends up being a roundabout. He's doomed and Bellow makes us care. <br /> <br />3) When you truly inhabit a character, the chosen mode of expression becomes an almost arbitrary distinction. Ostensibly this is an third person, past tense, omniscient narrator deal, but Bellow uses first person present tense, second person direct address, even stream of consciousness in order to get inside his protagonist's head. Bellow's approach is "by any means necessary." Seize the Day is reminiscent of some of the Leopold Bloom sections of Ulysses, particularly toward the end where Wilhelm starts to recede, almost as if he is being absorbed into the city that he will probably leave. <br /><br />This is really a fascinating little book that I imagine I'll come back to again. As a writer who obsesses over the mechanics of story, Seize the Day is wonderfully instructive. Also, Cynthia Ozick's introduction is awfully impressive. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4402493566200213731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_45857632" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45857632" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/739402-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1303912075p2/739402.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45857632?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/739402-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45857632" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3974274618567037950">It's been about a week since I finished this book, and have picked up two new books in the meantime, so my first thoughts are a bit hazy and lost to other curiosities. However, the thing about the book that has stuck with me - and will no doubt lead me to re-reading it in later years - is its examination of American ideals and the internal grapplings of a human soul. How wonderfully fresh and true this story remains today, over 50 years after it was written! Tommy, the novel's protagonist, must </span>
  <span id="freeText3974274618567037950" style="display:none">It's been about a week since I finished this book, and have picked up two new books in the meantime, so my first thoughts are a bit hazy and lost to other curiosities. However, the thing about the book that has stuck with me - and will no doubt lead me to re-reading it in later years - is its examination of American ideals and the internal grapplings of a human soul. How wonderfully fresh and true this story remains today, over 50 years after it was written! Tommy, the novel's protagonist, must come to terms with his life and his culture: corporatism and nepotism, financial loss, capitalist economics, morality, marriage, psychology, identity development, family dynamics. Tommy ruminates (and yes, sometimes fusses) over these subjects as he completes the daily routine. The interesting part is how, inside of Tommy, all these disparate subjects emerge as not so distinct after all, but entwined, his childhood feeding into his financial woes and so on. <br /><br />And there are no easy answers to any of Tommy's questions; Bellow muddies the waters a bit so that no character is a saint or a villian. In fact, I'm not sure, even after reading the novel, if Tamkin really was 'crazy' or a 'joke' and if the father is mean or level-headed. That's for the reader to struggle with, I suppose. <br /><br />On two side notes, someone mentioned in their review that Tommy is a loser, but I'm not sure I would agree. We've all probably been a little bit like Tommy at some point or another. At least, I can say personally, that I've worried myself into a hole, made mistakes, and wondered if I were doing the right thing. Secondly, I'd like to add that Bellow's writing is quite beautiful with moments of transcendence here and there. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3974274618567037950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2879518805" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2879518805" />
    <a title="James Tingle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66386578-james-tingle"><img alt="James Tingle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491430223p2/66386578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2879518805?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Tingle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Tingle" href="/user/show/66386578-james-tingle">James Tingle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2879518805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6876433051842869440"><br/>I started another of Bellow's novels, Herzog, years ago at Uni and gave up about one third through it as it all felt a bit slow and ponderous and although fairly well written, didn't grab me enough really. Maybe I'll give that one another go some time but with Seize the Day, I did manage to finish it at least, but it seemed hard work, especially as it's such a short book. Bellow seems to be quite a long winded chap and likes to say something over the stretch of a page that could be summed up in </span>
  <span id="freeText6876433051842869440" style="display:none"><br />I started another of Bellow's novels, Herzog, years ago at Uni and gave up about one third through it as it all felt a bit slow and ponderous and although fairly well written, didn't grab me enough really. Maybe I'll give that one another go some time but with Seize the Day, I did manage to finish it at least, but it seemed hard work, especially as it's such a short book. Bellow seems to be quite a long winded chap and likes to say something over the stretch of a page that could be summed up in a few sentences...fair enough if that's his way of writing I suppose, but I struggled to keep my interest going at times.<br />The plot is pretty minimal although that can really work with certain novels if the protagonist is particularly interesting- unfortunately, this guy was a bit of a grey man, a bit of a background player who slipped into the limelight by accident it almost felt like. The whole living with his Dad in the hotel thing was quite a nice idea, but not much seemed to come of that scenario and so that all fell flat as well...the two stars are really for the writing standard which I suppose was fair enough in places, but I'm not sure I'll be devouring the whole of Bellow's back catalog any time soon. I have a copy of Henderson the Rain King somewhere so might try that one day if I feel up to the task!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6876433051842869440" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10189197749517673685">The little novella ‘Seize the Day’ is rightly called a masterpiece. <br/>Like a modern Greek tragedy, we have in Tommy Wilhelm a protagonist who is facing the world closing in on him. Instead of shutting down, giving in or giving up, he feels very deeply. What he ask for is just a bare minimum of human understanding, of compassion<br/><br/>Sadly, his father, who is fiercely aloof, can’t provide this and regards him as a loser.   <span id="freeText10189197749517673685" style="display:none">The little novella ‘Seize the Day’ is rightly called a masterpiece. <br />Like a modern Greek tragedy, we have in Tommy Wilhelm a protagonist who is facing the world closing in on him. Instead of shutting down, giving in or giving up, he feels very deeply. What he ask for is just a bare minimum of human understanding, of compassion<br /><br />Sadly, his father, who is fiercely aloof, can’t provide this and regards him as a loser. He ex-wife demands even more alimony. He is swindled out of the little cash he has by a fast talking merchant of success.<br /><br />Having nowhere to turn, he absentmindedly joins a funeral possession, and upon seeing the body in the coffin, breaks down sobbing for all of us. A memorable conclusion. <br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_655763039" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/655763039" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6971227-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564001692p2/6971227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/655763039?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/6971227-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer655763039" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9310785093287015421">A deeply psychological novel, <i>Seize the Day</i> follows the middle-aged man in the life of a single day in New York City. "Psychological"... "single day"... Bellow's ante into the pool of single-day novels, alongside Joyce's <i>Ulysses</i> and Woolf's <i>Mrs. Dalloway</i>, is a much slimmer volume than its fellow one-day wonders, but carries perhaps no less of a whollop. The story follows Tommy Wilhelm, a middle-aged man, a failed actor, a failed salesman, a husband whose wife refuses him a divorce but takes his </span>
  <span id="freeText9310785093287015421" style="display:none">A deeply psychological novel, <i>Seize the Day</i> follows the middle-aged man in the life of a single day in New York City. "Psychological"... "single day"... Bellow's ante into the pool of single-day novels, alongside Joyce's <i>Ulysses</i> and Woolf's <i>Mrs. Dalloway</i>, is a much slimmer volume than its fellow one-day wonders, but carries perhaps no less of a whollop. The story follows Tommy Wilhelm, a middle-aged man, a failed actor, a failed salesman, a husband whose wife refuses him a divorce but takes his money all the same, and a son who - despite his proximity and frequency of visits with his father, remains philosophically and emotionally estranged. The story is loaded with irony, but in the ironical mass there are small nuggets of truth: strange and wonderful insights into what it means to be alive. According to <i>Herzog</i>: <i>Unexpected intrusions of beauty. That is what life is.</i> And <i>Seize the Day</i> is studded with unexpected intrusions of beauty.<br /><br />For a thorough and well-written discussion of the psychoanalytical edge of this very psychological novella, I defer to s.penkavich's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/474048575" rel="nofollow noopener">great review of this book</a>. Bellow is a highly psychological writer, in <i>Herzog</i> he re-invents the <i>Hamlet</i> dilemma of betrayal (Herzog's wife Madeline standing in for Gertrude, his friend Gersbach for Claudius), and in <i>Seize the Day</i> we see a textbook case of suppressed emotion (as spenk points out: a lack of 'orgastic' release). The novella opens with this supression:<blockquote>
  <i>When it came to concealing his troubles, Tommy Wilhelm was not less capable than the next fellow. So at least he thought and there was a certain amount of evidence to back him up.</i>
</blockquote>He conceals his troubles, but his troubles build and boil, wrack and rage beneath the surface, tear him away from the core outward. Wilhelm has self-proclaimedly 'reached the end of his rope' - he is in dire financial straits: unable to pay his rent for the month or support his demanding wife, he is without current employment, having dropped out of school to pursue of pipedream in Hollywood, then quit his job at the Rojax Corporation, where he worked as a salesman, when they divided up his territory. He has failed time and time again, and is won over by glamorous, but meretricious, opportunities which he pursues full-heartedly and abandons wallet-emptied. <br /><br />What strikes me as the reader is Wilhelm's imaginative vigor for what is <i>new</i>. Like the lovably-stupid Bouvard and Pécuchet, he endlessly pursues "new starts" - the consequence being that he remains in every endeavor a disappointed novice. At each junction of failure he faces the choice to 'crawl back' or to free-fall and hope to catch at a new opportunity before he reaches the ever-rising bottom: he chooses the later. It is a strange failure of human pride which prods us toward our own unhappiness. Our pride eludes us except for grand successes: each success raising and raising the bar, but only a small slip is enough for our pride to drop away beneath us. For Wilhelm, he has never, or rarely, experienced success, and each failure causes him to retreat more and more into himself, to hide away his failures in the shallow recesses of his remaining dignity. His socio-economic pressure compounds his troubles: his free-falls become more and more worrisome as he has less and less financial padding to cushion his imminent ruin.<br /><br />The most interesting and perplexing character of the novella, however, is Dr. Tamkin: Wilhelm's dubious and mysterious savior. A man of many tall tales, exaggerations, and flat-out lies, Tamkin offers Wilhelm the opportunity to invest in futures with him, guaranteeing him exorbitant profits. He is apparently a psychologist, an expert in hypnosis, an inventive man, but ultimately a shady figure, and likely a crook. He proselytizes his "seize the day" perspective, which borders on ironically religious, to Wilhelm, and frequently psychoanalyzes him to Wilhelm's dismay. The theme of time-perspective is prevalent throughout the novella, and reminded me of <a href="http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887324591204579036803469160852.html" rel="nofollow noopener">this article in the Wall Street Journal</a> which discussed how time-perspective affected our moods. According to the parlance of "time-perspective therapy" it would seem that Wilhelm has a strongly past-negative, and perhaps present-fatalist outlook on his life. He is very much mired with regrets of his past: of his choice to leave school to pursue acting, his choice to get married, his choice to leave his wife, his choice to leave his job, etc. He is constantly reminded of these failures throughout his day and lacks a healthy outlet for relieving his burden: his father will not discuss his son's failures or give him the sympathy which Wilhelm so badly desires, his wife will not talk to him, and he has little else in the way of human contacts. What is ironic in Tamkin's plea for Wilhelm to "seize the day" and live in the "here-and-now" is that the great failures of Wilhelm's life have been made under just that operating mentality. Despite his belabored decision processes, his choices are ultimately made in gut-reactions to opportunities. He follows his instantaneous feelings and momentous emotions, and they continually lead him to undesired paths which he fails to follow through on.<br /><br />The background of futures trading strikes a particular key in the time-perspective theme. Futures contracts, to be technical, are purchased agreements to exchange goods at a future date, at a predetermined price. When they say that they have purchased contracts for "December rye" they mean that they have purchased the right to sell some quantity of rye-commodity, at some date in December, at the fixed price of the contract (the price fluctuates over time before narrowing in on the exact value as the day of exchange approaches). Wilhelm, whose view of the future is wary if not pessimistic, is in a state of constant agitation throughout the day: he fears the movement in prices and feels impotent but attached to his remaining small savings. He feels that Tamkin is swindling him, but due to his own ignorance and his invested hope in the potential "easy-money" he does not withdraw his savings. Wilhelm is always looking for some easy escape from his condition; he feels that his life thus-far has been very difficult and that at some point he must receive some manna-like relief. Tamkin, he believes, could be that relief, though his hope and his skepticism are constantly at odds throughout the day as he is fed psychology, platitudes, and unbelievable stories.<br /><br />The final release of Wilhelm's pent-up emotions, at the funeral of an unknown young man (perhaps about the age of Wilhelm himself, hence the excess of emotion), is his ultimate emotional release, but leaves the reader wondering about his still-precarious position. His savings have been decimated, his father has cast him out and refused to help him, Tamkin has disappeared, and he remains jobless with the growing demands of his wife and children. He tells himself that he will return to Olive (his mistress) and invests in her his next "new start" - but a new start which does not renew him, does not renew the reader's faith that he will succeed or even get by. His position remains impossible. <i>Seize the Day</i> is not Bellow's greatest novel, but it is an excellent exercise in the novella format, which from the beginning introduces a tension and unease which pervades the 118 pages. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9310785093287015421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/5650849-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5650849-alan?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer489224602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5125455662112943571"> In my five-book intro to lit class taught at a community college, I often included this novella or Alice in Wonderland or Tom Sawyer or Slocum's Sailing Alone. You can see I was not adhering to either chronological or authorial order or pigeonholing.<br/> Bellow's short work evokes the America that Reagan and Bush advertise, the Market, the go-getters... New York in a word. (And, aware of current candidates from both US parties, I should add, the Losers.) Yet being Bellow, he raises essential questi</span>
  <span id="freeText5125455662112943571" style="display:none"> In my five-book intro to lit class taught at a community college, I often included this novella or Alice in Wonderland or Tom Sawyer or Slocum's Sailing Alone. You can see I was not adhering to either chronological or authorial order or pigeonholing.<br /> Bellow's short work evokes the America that Reagan and Bush advertise, the Market, the go-getters... New York in a word. (And, aware of current candidates from both US parties, I should add, the Losers.) Yet being Bellow, he raises essential questions, and he frames an America which derives from the Old World and hastens toward a Newer One.<br /> Among Bellow's best works, this is chiseled and funny, with an aura of in-betweeness, defeat, separation, joblessness-- to the point of a changed name. <br /> Tommy Wilhelm's sister paints. "She worked very hard, but there were fifty thousand people in NY with paints and brushes, each practically a law unto himself. It was the Tower of Babel in paint"(32).<br /> "Was Tamkin a lunatic, then? That sick Mr Perls at breakfast had said there was no easy way to tell the sane from the mad, and he was right about that in any big city and especially in NY…Every other man spoke in a language entirely his own, which he had figured out by private thinking.."(83).<br /> Tommy Wilhelm to Tamkin, "You can spend the entire second half of your life recovering from the mistakes of the first half."(86) Tamkin, "Talking always about spontaneous emotion and open, receptive and free impulses, he was about as expressive as a pincushion"(96).<br /> Tamkin sees himself as dealing only in facts. "The past is no good to us. The future….anxiety. Seize the Day." (66) [The next page is very funny, for my reader to find and enjoy.]<br /> Old men control everything. "Without needs. They don't need, therefore they have. I need, therefore I don't have" (102).<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="5125455662112943571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12828946386878463325" style="display:none">The story centers on a day in the life of Tommy Wilhelm, a failed actor, and failed sales representative. Now in his forties he is unemployed, separated from his wife (who refuses to agree to a divorce), and estranged from his father (who refuses to help him financially). He is also stuck with the same immaturity and lack of insight which has brought him to failure. He was convinced to invest in the commodities of lard in an attempt to make some quick money, but this also failed. Wilhelm experiences a day of reckoning as he is forced to examine his life.<br /><i>Oh, this was a day of reckoning. It was a day, he thought, on which, willing or not, he would take a good close look at the truth. He breathed hard and his misshapen hat came low upon his congested dark-blond face. A rude look.</i></span>

<div id="review_3537258702" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3537258702" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513962691p2/39346323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3537258702?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3537258702" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4580523315995510733">In 1956, the publication year of this book, a man wanting to be a movie star in Hollywood sorta halfway tries. Then fails. By now, we have read this story many times, and about many careers and failures. I loved this line: "He breathed in the sugar of the new morning." But, oh, that's awfully close to the lyrics of the new Harry Styles pop song "Watermelon Sugar" (you know-the next pretender to the throne of Frank Sinatra-but who knows where the next great pop star/actor will come from?. It's be</span>
  <span id="freeText4580523315995510733" style="display:none">In 1956, the publication year of this book, a man wanting to be a movie star in Hollywood sorta halfway tries. Then fails. By now, we have read this story many times, and about many careers and failures. I loved this line: "He breathed in the sugar of the new morning." But, oh, that's awfully close to the lyrics of the new Harry Styles pop song "Watermelon Sugar" (you know-the next pretender to the throne of Frank Sinatra-but who knows where the next great pop star/actor will come from?. It's been Lady Gaga for 12 years now, after all, owning the global dance stage as she brags on "Chromatica"-and deservedly so. And, like Gaga, Sinatra would have won MTV's new "Tricon" award for being able to do anything.) But our hero in Bellow's novel doesn't have the drive: yes, he has the looks, the face...but just wants the money. (In my case, I just want an electrical, flashing mask like Gaga and people standing around making my meat dress and my own manicurist and at least 3 personal trainers, but I'm too lazy to work for it.) This is a good book, but I hear Bellow is a great writer and I haven't found that great book yet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4580523315995510733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('67xdcI7irdbb+Hfs6fwucwVRl2437a4RLx3iK6SHxrVoBX3/xi/u3jd51kg7aDpcvnE0VvdNWj1ytqd4mmUnow==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Zuckerman, whose work was his life, is unable to write a line. Now his work is trekking from one doctor to another, but none can find a cause for the pain and nobody can assuage it. Zuckerman himself wonders if the pain can have been caused by his own books. And while he is wondering, his dependence on painkillers grows into an addiction to vodka, marijuana, and Percodan. \n\nThe Anatomy Lesson is a great comedy of illness written in what the English critic Hermione Lee has described as &quot;a manner at once...brash and thoughtful... lyrical and wry, which projects through comic expostulations and confessions...a knowing, humane authority.&quot; Los «otros textos» son una selección de los escritos que Pedro Almodóvar hiciera para diarios y revistas.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow">About Saul Bellow</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1470607356p3/4391.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow">Saul Bellow

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11844635757660795917">Saul Bellow was born in Lachine, Quebec, a suburb of Montreal, in 1915, and was raised in Chicago. He attended the University of Chicago, received his Bachelor's degree from Northwestern University in 1937, with honors in sociology and anthropology, did graduate work at the University of Wisconsin, and served in the Merchant Marines during World War II.<br/><br/>Mr. Bellow's first novel, <i>Dangling Man</i>, was p</span>
  <span id="freeText11844635757660795917" style="display:none">Saul Bellow was born in Lachine, Quebec, a suburb of Montreal, in 1915, and was raised in Chicago. He attended the University of Chicago, received his Bachelor's degree from Northwestern University in 1937, with honors in sociology and anthropology, did graduate work at the University of Wisconsin, and served in the Merchant Marines during World War II.<br /><br />Mr. Bellow's first novel, <i>Dangling Man</i>, was published in 1944, and his second, <i>The Victim</i>, in 1947. In 1948 he was awarded a Guggenheim Fellowship and spent two years in Paris and traveling in Europe, where he began <i>The Adventures of Augie March,</i>, which won the National Book Award for fiction in 1954. Later books include <i>Seize The Day</i> (1956), <i>Henderson The Rain King</i> (1959), <i>Herzog</i> (1964), <i>Mosby's Memoirs and Other Stories</i> (1968), and <i>Mr. Sammler's Planet</i> (1970). <i>Humboldt's Gift</i> (1975), was awarded the Pulitzer Prize. Both Herzog and Mr. Sammler's Planet were awarded the National Book Award for fiction. Mr. Bellow's first non-fiction work, <i>To Jerusalem and Back: A Personal Account</i>, published on October 25,1976, is his personal and literary record of his sojourn in Israel during several months in 1975.<br /><br />In 1965 Mr. Bellow was awarded the International Literary Prize for Herzog, becoming the first American to receive the prize. In January 1968 the Republic of France awarded him the Croix de Chevalier des Arts et Lettres, the highest literary distinction awarded by that nation to non-citizens, and in March 1968 he received the B'nai B'rith Jewish Heritage Award for "excellence in Jewish literature". In November 1976 he was awarded the America's Democratic Legacy Award of the Anti-Defamation League of B'nai B'rith, the first time this award was made to a literary personage.<br /><br />A playwright as well as a novelist, Mr. Bellow was the author of <i>The Last Analysis</i> and of three short plays, collectively entitled <i>Under the Weather</i>, which were produced on Broadway in 1966. He contributed fiction to <i>Partisan Review, Playboy, Harper's Bazaar, The New Yorker, Esquire</i>, and to literary quarterlies. His criticism appeared in <i>The New York Times Book Review</i>, <i>Horizon, Encounter, The New Republic, The New Leader</i>, and elsewhere. During the 1967 Arab-lsraeli conflict, he served as a war correspondent for <i>Newsday</i>. He taught at Bard College, Princeton University, and the University of Minnesota, and was a member of the Committee on Social Thought at the University of Chicago.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11844635757660795917" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
